### Revelation 7:11

**Text:** "And all the angels stood there in a circle around the throne and around the elders and the four living creatures, and they threw themselves down with their faces to the ground before the throne and worshiped God,"

**Interpretation:** Revelation 7:11 provides a vivid glimpse into the heavenly court, immediately following the declaration of salvation by the great multitude (Revelation 7:9-10). It describes the response of the celestial beings to this profound moment of worship, setting the stage for their own doxology in verse 12.

"All the angels" (καὶ πάντες οἱ ἄγγελοι) emphasizes the totality and universality of the angelic host, signifying a complete and unified response. They "were standing around" (εἱστήκεισαν κύκλῳ), indicating their established position of attendance and readiness in the divine court, encircling the central figures. "The throne" is the focal point of heaven, representing God's absolute sovereignty. "The elders" (τῶν πρεσβυτέρων) are the twenty-four elders (Revelation 4:4), often interpreted as representatives of the redeemed people of God. "The four living creatures" (τῶν τεσσάρων ζῴων) (Revelation 4:6-8) are often understood to represent all creation. The angels are positioned *around* these central figures, indicating a wider, encompassing circle of worshipers.

They "fell on their faces" (καὶ ἔπεσαν ἐπὶ τὰ πρόσωπα αὐτῶν), a profound and recurring posture of reverence, humility, and submission in the presence of divine majesty. This signifies ultimate adoration and recognition of God's supreme holiness. This act of prostration occurs "before the throne" (ἐνώπιον τοῦ θρόνου), emphasizing the object of their reverence.

Finally, they "worshiped God" (καὶ προσεκύνησαν τῷ θεῷ). The Greek verb *proskyneō* means "to prostrate oneself," "to do homage," or "to adore." Their worship is directed specifically to God, affirming His unique sovereignty, worthiness, and glory. This act of worship is a direct response to the declaration of salvation by the great multitude, affirming God's plan of redemption and His glory in accomplishing it.

**Theological Implications:**
*   **Universal Scope of Worship:** This verse expands the scope of worship beyond the redeemed multitude to include the entire heavenly host, painting a picture of cosmic adoration.
*   **Hierarchy and Unity in Heaven:** It illustrates a structured heavenly court, with God on the throne as the absolute center, surrounded by the elders and living creatures, and then the angels in an outer circle, all united in worship.
*   **Affirmation of God's Salvation:** The angelic worship is a direct affirmation of the salvation declared by the great multitude, underscoring that God's redemptive work is celebrated by both the saved and the entire heavenly realm.
*   **God's Absolute Worthiness:** The act of falling on faces and worshiping underscores God's absolute holiness, majesty, and worthiness to receive all praise, honor, and glory.

**Cross-references:**
- (Rev 4:10) the twenty-four elders throw themselves to the ground before the one who sits on the throne and worship the one who lives forever and ever, and they offer their crowns before his throne, saying:

### Revelation 7:12

**Text:** "saying, “Amen! Praise and glory, and wisdom and thanksgiving, and honor and power and strength be to our God for ever and ever. Amen!”"

**Interpretation:** Revelation 7:12 presents a profound declaration of praise from the heavenly host, serving as a climactic response to the vision of the great multitude standing before the throne and the Lamb. This verse encapsulates the essence of heavenly worship, articulating the boundless attributes of God that elicit such adoration.

This verse follows immediately after Revelation 7:11, where "all the angels stood there in a circle around the throne and around the elders and the four living creatures, and they threw themselves down with their faces to the ground before the throne and worshiped God." This scene unfolds after John witnesses the sealing of the 144,000 (7:1-8) and then a vast, innumerable multitude (7:9-10) crying out, "Salvation belongs to our God who sits on the throne, and to the Lamb!" (7:10). It is in response to this earthly multitude's acclamation of salvation that the entire angelic host, along with the elders and living creatures, prostrates itself and offers this magnificent sevenfold doxology to God.

The verse begins with an emphatic "Amen!" (ἀμήν - *amēn*), a solemn and emphatic endorsement of the preceding declaration of salvation. It sets a tone of absolute conviction for the praise that follows.

Then follows a sevenfold ascription of praise, a common feature in apocalyptic literature, representing completeness or perfection. Each term highlights a distinct aspect of God's character and worthiness:
*   **"Blessing" (ἡ εὐλογία - *hē eulogia*)**: Speaking well of God, acknowledging His goodness.
*   **"Glory" (ἡ δόξα - *hē doxa*)**: God's inherent majesty, splendor, and reputation.
*   **"Wisdom" (ἡ σοφία - *hē sophia*)**: God's perfect knowledge and skill in ordering creation and executing His plans.
*   **"Thanksgiving" (ἡ εὐχαριστία - *hē eucharistia*)**: Gratitude for God's gracious acts, especially salvation.
*   **"Honor" (ἡ τιμή - *hē timē*)**: High esteem and reverence due to God's supreme position.
*   **"Power" (ἡ δύναμις - *hē dynamis*)**: God's inherent ability, strength, and capacity to act.
*   **"Might" (ἡ ἰσχύς - *hē iskhys*)**: Robust, enduring strength and forceful capability.
The cumulative effect of these seven attributes is a comprehensive declaration of God's supreme worthiness.

This praise is directed "to our God" (τῷ Θεῷ ἡμῶν - *tō Theō hēmōn*), the one true God, the Father, who sits on the throne. The possessive "our" suggests a shared relationship. The doxology concludes with "forever and ever" (εἰς τοὺς αἰῶνας τῶν αἰώνων - *eis tous aiōnas tōn aiōnōn*), emphasizing the eternal duration of God's worthiness and the praise due to Him.

**Theological Implications:**
*   **God's Absolute Worthiness**: The verse unequivocally declares God's supreme worthiness of all praise, honor, and adoration.
*   **The Universality of Worship**: The scene depicts not only the redeemed multitude but also the entire heavenly host joining in worship, painting a picture of cosmic adoration.
*   **Worship as Response to Salvation**: The angelic praise is a direct response to the multitude's cry of salvation, highlighting that a primary motivation for worship is God's redemptive work.
*   **God's Sovereignty and Providence**: The attributes of wisdom, power, and might underscore God's absolute control and perfect plan.

**Cross-references:**
- (Rev 5:12-14) 12 all of whom were singing in a loud voice: “Worthy is the lamb who was killed to receive power and wealth and wisdom and might and honor and glory and praise!” 13 Then I heard every creature – in heaven, on earth, under the earth, in the sea, and all that is in them – singing: “To the one seated on the throne and to the Lamb be praise, honor, glory, and ruling power forever and ever!” 14 And the four living creatures were saying “Amen,” and the elders threw themselves to the ground and worshiped.

### Revelation 7:13

**Text:** "Then one of the elders asked me, “These dressed in long white robes – who are they and where have they come from?”"

**Interpretation:** Revelation 7:13 marks a pivotal moment in John's vision, shifting from the grand scene of universal worship to a direct, inquisitive interaction that prepares the way for a deeper understanding of the "great multitude." This verse initiates a dialogue between John and one of the heavenly elders, serving to illuminate the identity and origin of the previously described countless throng.

The verse immediately follows the magnificent sevenfold doxology offered by the angels, elders, and living creatures to God (7:11-12), which was itself a response to the great multitude's cry of salvation (7:9-10). John has just witnessed this innumerable company, "from every nation, tribe, people, and language," standing before the throne and the Lamb, "clothed in white robes and holding palm branches." The elder's question, therefore, is posed against the backdrop of this awe-inspiring vision of redeemed humanity, whose identity and significance are yet to be fully revealed to John.

The phrase "one of the elders" (εἷς ἐκ τῶν πρεσβυτέρων - *heis ek tōn presbyterōn*) refers to one of the twenty-four elders (Revelation 4:4), who often act as interpreters or mediators of revelation. The Greek "ἀπεκρίθη" (*apekrithē*), "spoke in response," indicates the elder is initiating a conversation prompted by the scene.

The elder asks, "Who are these, clothed in white robes" (Τίνες οὗτοί εἰσιν οἱ περιβεβλημένοι τὰς στολὰς τὰς λευκάς - *Tines houtoi eisin hoi peribeblēmenoi tas stolas tas leukas*). This rhetorical question directly addresses the identity of the vast multitude. The phrase "clothed in white robes" (perfect passive participle *peribeblēmenoi*) draws attention to their distinctive attire, which symbolizes purity, righteousness, victory, and divine favor (Revelation 3:4-5, 18; 6:11).

The second question, "and from where have they come?" (καὶ πόθεν ἦλθον - *kai pothen ēlthen*), probes the origin or source of this multitude. It seeks to understand their background, their journey, and the circumstances that led them to this glorious heavenly position, anticipating the explanation that they have come "out of the great tribulation" (7:14).

This interaction serves a pedagogical purpose for both John and the reader, prompting reflection and preparing for the profound answer in Revelation 7:14.

**Theological Implications:**
*   **The Identity of the Redeemed**: The elder's question sets the stage for the revelation of the multitude's identity as those who have come "out of the great tribulation" and "washed their robes and made them white in the blood of the Lamb" (7:14).
*   **Divine Revelation and Understanding**: The interaction demonstrates how God, through His heavenly agents, guides His servants into deeper understanding.
*   **The Significance of Appearance**: The emphasis on "white robes" indicates that outward appearance in the heavenly realm is deeply symbolic of one's spiritual state, purity, and relationship with God and the Lamb.
*   **The Journey of Faith**: The question "from where have they come?" implicitly acknowledges that the redeemed have a history, a journey through life involving tribulation and faithfulness.

**Cross-references:**
- (Rev 7:9) After these things I looked, and here was an enormous crowd that no one could count, made up of persons from every nation, tribe, people, and language, standing before the throne and before the Lamb dressed in long white robes, and with palm branches in their hands.
- (Rev 4:10) the twenty-four elders throw themselves to the ground before the one who sits on the throne and worship the one who lives forever and ever, and they offer their crowns before his throne, saying:
- (Rev 5:11) Then I looked and heard the voice of many angels in a circle around the throne, as well as the living creatures and the elders. Their number was ten thousand times ten thousand – thousands times thousands –
- (Rev 5:5) Then one of the elders said to me, “Stop weeping! Look, the Lion of the tribe of Judah, the root of David, has conquered; thus he can open the scroll and its seven seals.”
- (Rev 4:4) In a circle around the throne were twenty-four other thrones, and seated on those thrones were twenty-four elders. They were dressed in white clothing and had golden crowns on their heads.
- (John 7:28) Then Jesus, while teaching in the temple courts, cried out, “You both know me and know where I come from! And I have not come on my own initiative, but the one who sent me is true. You do not know him,
- (Judg 13:6) The woman went and said to her husband, “A man sent from God came to me! He looked like God’s angel – he was very awesome. I did not ask him where he came from, and he did not tell me his name.
- (Gen 16:8) He said, “Hagar, servant of Sarai, where have you come from, and where are you going?” She replied, “I’m running away from my mistress, Sarai.”

### Revelation 7:14

**Text:** "So I said to him, “My lord, you know the answer.” Then he said to me, “These are the ones who have come out of the great tribulation. They have washed their robes and made them white in the blood of the Lamb!"

**Interpretation:** Revelation 7:14 provides a pivotal explanation for the identity and experience of the "great multitude" introduced in the preceding verses. This verse states, "And he said to me, 'These are the ones coming out of the great tribulation. They have washed their robes and made them white in the blood of the Lamb.'" This response from one of the twenty-four elders to John's unspoken question (Rev 7:13) clarifies the origin and means of purification for this vast, international assembly.

The "these" (οὗτοί) in 7:14 refers directly to the "enormous crowd that no one could count, made up of persons from every nation, tribe, people, and language" described in 7:9. This multitude stands "before the throne and before the Lamb," holding palm branches and crying out praises to God and the Lamb (7:9-10).

The phrase "the great tribulation" (τῆς θλίψεως τῆς μεγάλης) is a central point of interpretive divergence. *Thlipsis* (θλίψις) denotes pressure, distress, affliction, or persecution. Interpretations include:
1.  **Preterist:** Specific historical events like the Jewish War (70 AD) or Roman persecution.
2.  **Historicist:** A prolonged period of suffering for the church throughout history.
3.  **Futurist:** A specific, intense future period of suffering before Christ's second coming.
4.  **Idealist/Symbolic:** The ongoing trials and persecutions experienced by the church throughout the inter-advent age.
Regardless, it refers to a period of intense suffering and testing for God's people. The Greek participle "coming out of" (οἱ ἐρχόμενοι ἐκ) can imply either an ongoing process or a completed action, suggesting they have persevered through or been delivered from this affliction.

The powerful and paradoxical imagery, "Washed their robes and made them white in the blood of the Lamb" (ἔπλυναν τὰς στολὰς αὐτῶν καὶ ἐλεύκαναν αὐτὰς ἐν τῷ αἵματι τοῦ ἀρνίου), is central. The "robes" (*stolas*) symbolize purity, righteousness, and victory. "Washed" and "made white" indicate a decisive cleansing. The paradox is that blood, which typically stains, is the agent of cleansing. "The Lamb" (*tou arniou*) is Jesus Christ, emphasizing His sacrificial death. "Blood" (*haimati*) symbolizes His atoning sacrifice. This signifies that the purity and righteousness of the redeemed are achieved solely through Christ's atoning sacrifice, not human merit. For those "coming out of the great tribulation," this also implies martyrdom or enduring severe suffering for their faith, leading to vindication and purification.

**Theological Implications:**
*   **Salvation and Atonement:** Salvation, purification, and access to God are solely by grace through Christ's atoning sacrifice.
*   **Suffering and Perseverance:** Suffering and tribulation are an expected part of the journey for God's people. They are brought *through* it, emphasizing divine preservation.
*   **Identity of God's People:** The multitude are those who have endured suffering for Christ and whose righteousness is from His sacrifice, representing the universal church.
*   **Assurance and Hope:** This vision offers immense assurance that those who suffer for Christ will be vindicated, purified, and brought into eternal communion with God.

**Cross-references:**

### Revelation 7:15

**Text:** "For this reason they are before the throne of God, and they serve him day and night in his temple, and the one seated on the throne will shelter them."

**Interpretation:** Revelation 7:15 presents a profound vision of the ultimate blessedness and secure destiny of the "great multitude" previously identified in Revelation 7:9-14. This verse acts as a culmination of the preceding description, detailing the privileges and protection granted to those who have endured and been purified.

The verse immediately follows the identification of "these" (οὗτοι – houtoi) as those "who have come out of the great tribulation" (ἐκ τῆς θλίψεως τῆς μεγάλης – ek tēs thlipseōs tēs megalēs) and "have washed their robes and made them white in the blood of the Lamb" (ἔπλυναν τὰς στολὰς αὐτῶν καὶ ἐλεύκαναν αὐτὰς ἐν τῷ αἵματι τοῦ ἀρνίου – eplynan tas stolas autōn kai eleukanan autas en tō haimati tou arniou). Their standing "before the throne and before the Lamb" (ἐνώπιον τοῦ θρόνου καὶ ἐνώπιον τοῦ ἀρνίου – enōpion tou thronou kai enōpion tou arniou) signifies their vindication, acceptance, and direct access to God's presence, a privilege earned through Christ's sacrifice and their faithful endurance. Verse 15 then explains the *consequence* of this purification and perseverance.

The verse begins with "Therefore" (διὰ τοῦτο – dia touto), explicitly linking their blessed state to their past actions and purification.
1.  **"they are before the throne of God"**: This reiterates their privileged position, emphasizing their direct, unhindered access to the divine presence, signifying ultimate acceptance and vindication.
2.  **"and serve him day and night in his temple"**: The term "serve" (λατρεύουσιν – latreuousin) connotes worship and priestly service, indicating active, continuous devotion. "Day and night" (ἡμέρας καὶ νυκτὸς – hēmeras kai nyktos) is an idiom for ceaseless activity, emphasizing the eternal nature of their worship. "In his temple" (ἐν τῷ ναῷ αὐτοῦ – en tō naō autou) refers to the inner sanctuary, suggesting intimate and holy proximity to God.
3.  **"and he who sits on the throne will shelter them with his presence"**: The Greek verb "σκηνώσει" (skēnōsei) means "will spread his tent over them" or "will tabernacle over them." This imagery alludes to the Old Testament Tabernacle, where God's presence dwelt among His people (Exodus 25:8). It signifies God's personal, intimate, and protective presence, guaranteeing their safety and well-being.

**Broader Context and Theological Implications:**
*   **Worship as the Ultimate End:** This verse reinforces Revelation's theme of worship as the ultimate purpose and activity of the redeemed.
*   **Divine Protection and Provision:** The promise that God "will shelter them with his presence" signifies the ultimate fulfillment of God's covenant promise to dwell among them.
*   **Priestly People:** By serving "in his temple," the multitude takes on a priestly role, fulfilling the Old Testament vision of Israel as a "kingdom of priests" (Exodus 19:6).
*   **Fulfillment of Old Testament Hopes:** The "tabernacling" imagery connects to prophecies about God dwelling with His people in a new covenant (Ezekiel 37:27).
*   **Contrast with Earthly Suffering:** The glorious, secure, and worshipful state described provides a powerful contrast to the suffering they experienced during the tribulation.

**Cross-references:**

- (Isa 4:5-6) 5 Then the Lord will create over all of Mount Zion and over its convocations a cloud and smoke by day and a bright flame of fire by night; indeed a canopy will accompany the Lord’s glorious presence. 6 By day it will be a shelter to provide shade from the heat, as well as safety and protection from the heavy downpour.
- (Rev 21:3-4) 3 And I heard a loud voice from the throne saying: “Look! The residence of God is among human beings. He will live among them, and they will be his people, and God himself will be with them. 4 He will wipe away every tear from their eyes, and death will not exist any more – or mourning, or crying, or pain, for the former things have ceased to exist.”
- (John 1:14) Now the Word became flesh and took up residence among us. We saw his glory – the glory of the one and only, full of grace and truth, who came from the Father.

### Revelation 7:16

**Text:** "They will never go hungry or be thirsty again, and the sun will not beat down on them, nor any burning heat,"

**Interpretation:** Revelation 7:16 describes the blessed and secure state of the "great multitude" (introduced in 7:9) who stand before God's throne, having come out of the great tribulation. This verse offers a profound vision of divine provision, protection, and comfort for the redeemed, contrasting their past suffering with their eternal rest.

1.  **"They shall hunger no more, neither thirst any more"**: This promise speaks to the definitive end of physical deprivation. In biblical thought, hunger and thirst are often metaphors for spiritual longing and dissatisfaction (Amos 8:11-12; John 6:35; 4:14). Thus, this signifies not just the absence of physical need but the complete and eternal satisfaction of the soul in God's presence, where all spiritual longings are met. The Greek phrase *peinasousin eti oude dipsēsousin eti* uses a strong negation, emphasizing the absolute and permanent cessation of these needs.

2.  **"neither shall the sun strike them, nor any scorching heat"**: This imagery promises protection from the harsh elements. "Scorching heat" (*kauma* in Greek) is often associated with divine judgment and intense suffering in apocalyptic literature (Revelation 16:8-9). Therefore, this signifies an end to all forms of tribulation, suffering, and the destructive forces of judgment. It assures the redeemed of God's complete shelter and care, shielding them from any further harm or distress. The double negative *oude mē* further reinforces the absolute certainty.

**Contextual and Theological Significance:**
This verse is a powerful declaration of the ultimate blessedness awaiting those who endure tribulation and remain faithful. It stands in stark contrast to the suffering and persecution that the "great multitude" has experienced (7:14). The promises of no more hunger, thirst, or scorching heat are direct reversals of the hardships often faced by God's people throughout history, and particularly during the "great tribulation" depicted in Revelation.

The verse sets the stage for the subsequent promise in Revelation 7:17, which explicitly states that "the Lamb in the midst of the throne will shepherd them, and will guide them to springs of living water." This connection is crucial: the cessation of suffering and the provision of ultimate comfort are not abstract promises but are actively mediated by the Lamb, Jesus Christ, who is both the sacrificed redeemer and the victorious shepherd. He is the source of this eternal satisfaction and protection.

In essence, Revelation 7:16 paints a picture of perfect peace, security, and divine provision for the redeemed. It assures them that their past suffering is over, and they are now under the direct, loving care of God, experiencing complete fulfillment and protection in His eternal presence.

**Cross-references:**
- (Isa 49:10) They will not be hungry or thirsty; the sun’s oppressive heat will not beat down on them, for one who has compassion on them will guide them; he will lead them to springs of water.
- (Matt 5:6) “Blessed are those who hunger and thirst for righteousness, for they will be satisfied.
- (Isa 65:13) So this is what the Sovereign Lord says: “Look, my servants will eat, but you will be hungry! Look, my servants will drink, but you will be thirsty! Look, my servants will rejoice, but you will be humiliated!
- (Ps 121:6) The sun will not harm you by day, or the moon by night.
- (Rev 21:4) He will wipe away every tear from their eyes, and death will not exist any more – or mourning, or crying, or pain, for the former things have ceased to exist.”
- (Jas 1:11) For the sun rises with its heat and dries up the meadow; the petal of the flower falls off and its beauty is lost forever. So also the rich person in the midst of his pursuits will wither away.
- (John 4:14) But whoever drinks some of the water that I will give him will never be thirsty again, but the water that I will give him will become in him a fountain of water springing up to eternal life.”
- (Luke 6:21) “Blessed are you who hunger now, for you will be satisfied. “Blessed are you who weep now, for you will laugh.
- (Luke 1:53) he has filled the hungry with good things, and has sent the rich away empty.
- (Mark 4:17) But they have no root in themselves and do not endure. Then, when trouble or persecution comes because of the word, immediately they fall away.
- (Mark 4:6) When the sun came up it was scorched, and because it did not have sufficient root, it withered.
- (Matt 13:21) But he has no root in himself and does not endure; when trouble or persecution comes because of the word, immediately he falls away.
- (Matt 13:6) But when the sun came up, they were scorched, and because they did not have sufficient root, they withered.
- (Jonah 4:8) When the sun began to shine, God sent a hot east wind. So the sun beat down on Jonah’s head, and he grew faint. So he despaired of life, and said, “I would rather die than live!”
- (Isa 41:17) The oppressed and the poor look for water, but there is none; their tongues are parched from thirst. I, the Lord, will respond to their prayers; I, the God of Israel, will not abandon them.
- (Isa 32:2) Each of them will be like a shelter from the wind and a refuge from a rainstorm; like streams of water in a dry region and like the shade of a large cliff in a parched land.
- (Isa 25:4) For you are a protector for the poor, a protector for the needy in their distress, a shelter from the rainstorm, a shade from the heat. Though the breath of tyrants is like a winter rainstorm,
- (Isa 4:5-6) 5 Then the Lord will create over all of Mount Zion and over its convocations a cloud and smoke by day and a bright flame of fire by night; indeed a canopy will accompany the Lord’s glorious presence. 6 By day it will be a shelter to provide shade from the heat, as well as safety and protection from the heavy downpour.
- (Song 1:6) Do not stare at me because I am dark, for the sun has burned my skin. My brothers were angry with me; they made me the keeper of the vineyards. Alas, my own vineyard I could not keep!
- (Ps 143:6) I spread my hands out to you in prayer; my soul thirsts for you in a parched land. (Selah)
- (Ps 63:1) A psalm of David, written when he was in the Judean wilderness. O God, you are my God! I long for you! My soul thirsts for you, my flesh yearns for you, in a dry and parched land where there is no water.
- (Ps 42:2) I thirst for God, for the living God. I say, “When will I be able to go and appear in God’s presence?”

### Revelation 7:17

**Text:** "because the Lamb in the middle of the throne will shepherd them and lead them to springs of living water, and God will wipe away every tear from their eyes.”"

**Interpretation:** Revelation 7:17 serves as the climactic conclusion to the vision of the great multitude, offering a profound glimpse into the ultimate blessedness and security of God's redeemed people. This verse encapsulates the divine care and provision awaiting those who have endured tribulation and been washed in the blood of the Lamb.

This verse builds upon the promises in 7:16, detailing the specific actions of the Lamb and God in providing ultimate comfort and sustenance. It draws on rich theological concepts: the Lamb as Shepherd (John 10:11-16; Psalm 23), "living waters" (John 4:10-14; 7:37-38), and God wiping away tears (Isaiah 25:8; Revelation 21:4).

1.  **"For the Lamb who is in the midst of the throne" (ὅτι τὸ ἀρνίον τὸ ἀνὰ μέσον τοῦ θρόνου - *hoti to arnion to ana meson tou thronou*)**: "The Lamb" (*to arnion*) is Christ, emphasizing His sacrificial nature alongside His power. "In the midst of the throne" places the Lamb at the very center, underscoring His co-equality with God and His central, active role in divine governance and salvation.

2.  **"Will shepherd them" (ποιμανεῖ αὐτούς - *poimanei autous*)**: The future indicative verb `ποιμαίνω` (*poimainō*) means "to shepherd, tend a flock, rule." This imagery, deeply rooted in the Old Testament (Psalm 23:1; Isaiah 40:11), signifies active, protective, and nurturing care. The paradox of the slain Lamb becoming the ruling Shepherd is central to Revelation's Christology.

3.  **"And will guide them to springs of waters of life" (καὶ ὁδηγήσει αὐτοὺς ἐπὶ πηγὰς ὑδάτων ζωῆς - *kai hodēgēsei autous epi pēgas hydatōn zōēs*)**: "Will guide" (`ὁδηγήσει` - *hodēgēsei*) reinforces the active, directional role of the Shepherd. "Springs of waters of life" (`πηγὰς ὑδάτων ζωῆς`) implies abundant, continuous, and fresh sources of spiritual sustenance, eternal vitality, and complete satisfaction for the redeemed, echoing Jesus' words about "living water" (John 4:10-14).

4.  **"And God will wipe away every tear from their eyes" (καὶ ἐξαλείψει ὁ θεὸς πᾶν δάκρυον ἐκ τῶν ὀφθαλμῶν αὐτῶν - *kai exaleipsei ho theos pan dakryon ek tōn ophthalmōn autōn*)**: "Will wipe away" (`ἐξαλείψει` - *exaleipsei*) is a strong verb meaning "to blot out, erase completely," emphasizing the finality and completeness of the eradication of sorrow. "Every tear" (`πᾶν δάκρυον`) stresses the totality of this divine act. It is ultimately God Himself who performs this final, ultimate act of comfort, connecting to similar promises in Isaiah 25:8 and Revelation 21:4.

**Significance:** Revelation 7:17 offers a profound vision of eschatological peace and comfort. It portrays the Lamb in His dual role as both the sacrificial victim and the sovereign Shepherd, actively caring for His redeemed people. He leads them to an inexhaustible source of life-giving spiritual sustenance. The ultimate promise, delivered by God Himself, is the complete and final removal of all sorrow and suffering. This verse provides immense hope and assurance to believers, particularly those undergoing tribulation, that their suffering is temporary and will be superseded by eternal joy and divine intimacy.

**Cross-references:**
- (Rev 21:4) He will wipe away every tear from their eyes, and death will not exist any more – or mourning, or crying, or pain, for the former things have ceased to exist.”
- (John 4:14) But whoever drinks some of the water that I will give him will never be thirsty again, but the water that I will give him will become in him a fountain of water springing up to eternal life.”
- (John 10:11) “I am the good shepherd. The good shepherd lays down his life for the sheep.
- (Isa 25:8) he will swallow up death permanently. The Sovereign Lord will wipe away the tears from every face, and remove his people’s disgrace from all the earth. Indeed, the Lord has announced it!
- (Ps 23:1-2) 1 A psalm of David. The Lord is my shepherd, I lack nothing. 2 He takes me to lush pastures, he leads me to refreshing water.
- (Rev 21:6) He also said to me, “It is done! I am the Alpha and the Omega, the beginning and the end. To the one who is thirsty I will give water free of charge from the spring of the water of life.
- (Rev 22:1) Then the angel showed me the river of the water of life – water as clear as crystal – pouring out from the throne of God and of the Lamb,

- (John 7:37-38) 37 On the last day of the feast, the greatest day, Jesus stood up and shouted out, “If anyone is thirsty, let him come to me, and 38 let the one who believes in me drink. Just as the scripture says, ‘From within him will flow rivers of living water.’”

- (Rev 5:6) Then I saw standing in the middle of the throne and of the four living creatures, and in the middle of the elders, a Lamb that appeared to have been killed. He had seven horns and seven eyes, which are the seven spirits of God sent out into all the earth.

You know that I love you.” Jesus replied, “Feed my sheep.
